<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_equipment">Organization and equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army_Air_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Organization and equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Command_structure">Command structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army_Air_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Command structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the end of World War II, the USAAF had created 16 <b><a href="/wiki/Numbered_Air_Force" title="Numbered Air Force">numbered air forces</a></b> (<i>First</i> through <i>Fifteenth</i> and <i>Twentieth</i>) distributed worldwide to prosecute the war, plus a general air force within the continental United States to support the whole and provide air defense.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[n 22]</a></sup> The latter was formally organized as the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Air_Forces" title="Continental Air Forces">Continental Air Forces</a> and activated on 15 December 1944, although it did not formally take jurisdiction of its component air forces until the end of the war in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[n 23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Half of the numbered air forces were created <i>de novo</i> as the service expanded during the war. Some grew out of earlier commands as the service expanded in size and hierarchy (for example, the <i>V Air Support Command</i> became the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Forces_Central_Command" title="United States Air Forces Central Command">Ninth Air Force</a> in April 1942),<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[n 24]</a></sup> and higher echelons such as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Strategic_Air_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Strategic Air Forces">United States Strategic Air Forces</a> (USSTAF) in Europe<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[n 25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Strategic_Air_Forces_in_the_Pacific" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Strategic Air Forces in the Pacific">U.S. Strategic Air Forces in the Pacific</a> became necessary to control the whole.</p>
<p>A subordinate organizational tier within the numbered air force, the <i>operational command</i>, was created to segregate units of similar functions (fighters and bombers) for administrative control. The numbering of the operational command was designated by the Roman numeral of its parent numbered air force. For instance, the Eighth Air Force listed the VIII Bomber Command and the <a href="/wiki/VIII_Fighter_Command" title="VIII Fighter Command">VIII Fighter Command</a> as subordinate operational commands. Roman numbered commands within numbered air forces also included "support," "base," and other services commands to support the operational units, such as the VIII Air Force Service and VIII Air Force Composite Commands<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[n 26]</a></sup> also part of Eighth Air Force during its history. The use of Roman-numeral commands was nonstandard within the AAF; the <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Air_Force" title="Tenth Air Force">Tenth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Air_Force" title="Fourteenth Air Force">Fourteenth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifteenth Air Force">Fifteenth Air Forces</a> did not field subordinate commands during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[n 27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eight <i>air divisions</i> served as an additional layer of <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control" title="Command and control">command and control</a> for the vast organization, capable of acting independently if the need arose.</p>
<p>Inclusive within the air forces, commands and divisions were administrative headquarters called <i><a href="/wiki/Wing_(military_aviation_unit)" title="Wing (military aviation unit)">wings</a></i> to control <i>groups</i> (operational units; see section below). As the number of groups increased, the number of wings needed to control them multiplied, with 91 ultimately activated, 69 of which were still active at the end of the war. As part of the Air Service and Air Corps, wings had been composite organizations, that is, composed of groups with different types of missions. Most of the wings of World War II, however, were composed of groups with like functions (denoted as <i>bombardment</i>, <i>fighter</i>, <i>reconnaissance</i>, <i>training</i>, <i>antisubmarine</i>, <i>troop carrier</i>, and <i>replacement</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-maurer8_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer8-104">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[n 28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The six <i>support commands</i> organized between March 1941 and April 1942 to support and supply the numbered air forces remained on the same <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_command" class="mw-redirect" title="Chain of command">chain of command</a> echelon as the numbered air forces, under the direct control of Headquarters Army Air Forces. At the end of 1942 and again in the spring of 1943 the AAF listed nine support commands before it began a process of consolidation that streamlined the number to five at the end of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png/220px-Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png/330px-Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="383" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command_-_Patch.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
AAF Training Command patch</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These commands were:</p>
<dl>
<dt>Support commands active on 15 September 1945</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Air_Transport_Command_(United_States_Air_Force)" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Transport Command (United States Air Force)">Air Transport Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[n 29]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_Training_Command" title="Army Air Forces Training Command">Army Air Forces Training Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[n 30]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Air_Technical_Service_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Technical Service Command">Air Technical Service Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[n 31]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_School_of_Applied_Tactics" title="Army Air Forces School of Applied Tactics">Army Air Forces Center</a><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[n 32]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/w/index.php?title=Army_Air_Forces_Personnel_Distribution_Command&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Army Air Forces Personnel Distribution Command (page does not exist)">Army Air Forces Personnel Distribution Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[n 33]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>Discontinued or merged support commands</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_Flying_Training_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Army Air Forces Flying Training Command">Army Air Forces Flying Training Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[n 34]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_Technical_Training_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Army Air Forces Technical Training Command">Army Air Forces Technical Training Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[n 35]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Air_Service_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Service Command">Air Service Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[n 36]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>Materiel Command<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[n 37]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Proving_Ground_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Proving Ground Command">Proving Ground Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[n 38]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_Troop_Carrier_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Army Air Forces Troop Carrier Command">I Troop Carrier Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[n 39]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>I Concentration Command<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[n 40]</a></sup></dd>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Forces_Antisubmarine_Command" title="Army Air Forces Antisubmarine Command">Antisubmarine Command</a><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[n 41]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>Flight Control Command<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[n 42]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combat_units">Combat units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army_Air_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Combat units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg/220px-Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg/330px-Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg/440px-Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1456" data-file-height="1928" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oer_the_ramparts_we_watch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
USAAF recruiting poster.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The primary combat unit of the Army Air Forces for both administrative and tactical purposes was the <a href="/wiki/Group_(air_force)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group (air force)">group</a>, an organization of three or four flying <a href="/wiki/Squadron_(aviation)" title="Squadron (aviation)">squadrons</a><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[n 43]</a></sup> and attached or organic ground support elements, which was the rough equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Regiment" title="Regiment">regiment</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ground_Forces" title="Army Ground Forces">Army Ground Forces</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-goss58_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goss58-123">[80]</a></sup> The Army Air Forces fielded a total of 318 combat groups at some point during World War II, with an operational force of 243 combat groups in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer7_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer7-124">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Service" title="United States Army Air Service">Air Service</a> and its successor the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Corps" title="United States Army Air Corps">Air Corps</a> had established 15 permanent combat groups between 1919 and 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer7_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer7-124">[81]</a></sup> With the buildup of the combat force beginning 1 February 1940, the Air Corps expanded from 15 to 30 groups by the end of the year. On 7 December 1941 the number of activated combat groups had reached 67, with 49 still within the Continental United States. Of the CONUS groups (the "strategic reserve"), 21 were engaged in operational training or still being organized and were unsuitable for deployment.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[n 44]</a></sup> Of the 67 combat groups, 26 were classified as bombardment: 13 <i>Heavy Bomb</i> groups (<a href="/wiki/B-17_Flying_Fortress" class="mw-redirect" title="B-17 Flying Fortress">B-17 Flying Fortress</a> and <a href="/wiki/B-24_Liberator" class="mw-redirect" title="B-24 Liberator">B-24 Liberator</a>), and the rest <i>Medium</i> and <i>Light</i> groups (<a href="/wiki/B-25_Mitchell" class="mw-redirect" title="B-25 Mitchell">B-25 Mitchell</a>, <a href="/wiki/B-26_Marauder" class="mw-redirect" title="B-26 Marauder">B-26 Marauder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/A-20_Havoc" class="mw-redirect" title="A-20 Havoc">A-20 Havoc</a>). The balance of the force included 26 <i>Pursuit</i> groups (renamed <i><a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter group</a></i> in May 1942), 9 <i>Observation</i> (renamed <i>Reconnaissance</i>) groups, and 6 <i>Transport</i> (renamed <i>Troop Carrier</i> or <i>Combat Cargo</i>) groups.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer8_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer8-104">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[n 45]</a></sup> After the operational deployment of the <a href="/wiki/B-29_Superfortress" class="mw-redirect" title="B-29 Superfortress">B-29 Superfortress</a> bomber, <i>Very Heavy Bombardment</i> units were added to the force array.</p>
<p>In the first half of 1942 the Army Air Forces expanded rapidly as the necessity of a much larger air force than planned was immediately realized. Authorization for the total number of combat groups required to fight the war nearly doubled in February to 115. In July it jumped to 224, and a month later to 273. When the U.S. entered the war, however, the number of groups actually trained to a standard of combat proficiency had barely surpassed the total originally authorized by the first expansion program in 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[84]</a></sup> The extant training establishment, in essence a "self-training" system, was inadequate in assets, organization, and <a href="/wiki/Pedagogy" title="Pedagogy">pedagogy</a> to train units wholesale. Individual training of freshly-minted pilots occupied an inordinate amount of the available time to the detriment of unit proficiency. The ever-increasing numbers of new groups being formed had a deleterious effect on operational training and threatened to overwhelm the capacity of the old Air Corps groups to provide experienced cadres or to absorb graduates of the expanded training program to replace those transferred. Since 1939 the overall level of experience among the combat groups had fallen to such an extent that when the demand for replacements in combat was factored in, the entire operational training system was threatened.<sup id="cite_ref-cc600_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cc600-130">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_roundel_1943-1947.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/US_roundel_1943-1947.svg/150px-US_roundel_1943-1947.svg.png" width="150" height="83" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/US_roundel_1943-1947.svg/225px-US_roundel_1943-1947.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/US_roundel_1943-1947.svg/300px-US_roundel_1943-1947.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1270" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_roundel_1943-1947.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
USAAF insignia from July 1943 to January 1947</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To avoid this probable crisis, an Operational Training Unit (OTU) system was adopted as it had been by the RAF. Under the American OTU concept, certain experienced groups were authorized as overstrength "parent" groups. A parent group (OTU unit) provided approximately 20% of its seasoned personnel as cadre to a newly activated, or "satellite," group. Cadres detached to the newly activated satellite group were first provided with special instruction on their training responsibilities, initially by the responsible air forces, but after 9 October 1942, by the <a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Force_School_of_Applied_Tactics" class="mw-redirect" title="Army Air Force School of Applied Tactics">Army Air Force School of Applied Tactics</a> (AAFSAT) to standardize curriculum and instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-cc600_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cc600-130">[85]</a></sup> New graduates of training schools fleshed out the satellite group and also restored the parent group to its overstrength size. The parent group was responsible for the organization and training of its satellite, normally a process six months in length that began the day of detachment of the cadre, the first half of the process bringing the new unit up to strength, the second half devoted to flying training, with the final six weeks concentrating on fighting as a unit.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The plan was first adopted in February 1942 by the AFCC's <a href="/wiki/Second_Air_Force" title="Second Air Force">Second</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_Air_Force" title="Third Air Force">Third Air Forces</a>, which had only training responsibilities during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[87]</a></sup> The creation of an "operating staff" in the 9 March 1942 reorganization of the AAF and the dissolution of the AFCC halted the planned establishment of an Operational Training Command to oversee the program. Spaatz, last commanding general of the AFCC, was temporarily given supervisory responsibility for OTU while the new directorates were brought up to speed,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[88]</a></sup> but after April 1942 the sub-directorates having jurisdiction over the training<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[n 46]</a></sup> tended to tell the air forces not only what to do, but how to do it. When the operating staff and its directorates were abolished in March 1943, control of OTU/RTU activities was placed under the <i>Assistant Chief of Air Staff, Training</i> and administered by the <i>Unit Training Division</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1942 the plan was extended to all four continental air forces but not until early 1943 were most developmental problems resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[n 47]</a></sup> Before the system matured, each air force became predominant in one type of OTU training, heavy bomber in the Second Air Force, medium and light bomber in the Third, and fighters in the <a href="/wiki/First_Air_Force" title="First Air Force">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Air_Force" title="Fourth Air Force">Fourth</a> (which also had an air defense responsibility), but eventually both fighter and bombardment OTU were conducted in all four. When the bulk of new groups (and several parent groups) had been sent overseas, replacement training (RTU)<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[n 48]</a></sup> took precedence over OTU and except for three B-29 groups,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[n 49]</a></sup> no new satellites were formed after October 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[91]</a></sup> In December 1943, 56 groups were assigned to the strategic reserve as OTU parent units or RTUs,<sup id="cite_ref-sd7_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd7-141">[92]</a></sup> and the AAF had reached its maximum size, 269 groups. 136 were deployed overseas and of those still in the United States, 77 were also being organized and trained for overseas deployment. In the spring of 1944 all operational and replacement training was reassigned to "base units" of the respective CONUS air forces,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[n 50]</a></sup> resulting in the inactivation or disbanding between 31 March and 1 May 1944 of 49 OTU/RTU groups, which reduced the number of active groups to 218. However, additional groups were formed in the following months to bring the AAF to its final wartime structure.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer7_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer7-124">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sd7_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd7-141">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1945 the AAF fielded 243 combat groups:</p>
<ul>
<li>125 <a href="/wiki/USAAF_bombardment_group" class="mw-redirect" title="USAAF bombardment group">Bombardment groups</a> (25 Very Heavy, 72 Heavy, 20 Medium, and 8 Light);</li>
<li>71 Fighter groups;<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[n 51]</a></sup></li>
<li>29 Troop Carrier and Combat Cargo groups;<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[n 52]</a></sup></li>
<li>13 Reconnaissance groups;<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[n 53]</a></sup> and</li>
<li>5 Composite groups.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer8_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer8-104">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[n 54]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Between the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Normandy" title="Invasion of Normandy">Invasion of Normandy</a> in June 1944 and <a href="/wiki/VE_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="VE Day">VE Day</a> in 1945, 149 combat groups fought against Germany, while by August 1945, when all combat operations ended, 86 groups were deployed in the Pacific and Far East. The European force was then either performing occupation duties or re-deploying to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer7_124-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer7-124">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sd7_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd7-141">[92]</a></sup> With the partial demobilization of the forces in Europe, the total of active groups in the AAF had been reduced to 213. Nearly all of the discontinued units were heavy bombardment groups (B-17 and B-24), which numbered only 35 at the war's end. The remainder had been inactivated or redesignated as very heavy bombardment (B-29).<sup id="cite_ref-sd7_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sd7-141">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The basic permanent organization of the AAF for combat elements was the squadron.<sup id="cite_ref-goss58_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goss58-123">[80]</a></sup> 1,226 combat squadrons were active in the USAAF between 7 December 1941 and 2 September 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[n 55]</a></sup> At the end of hostilities in 1945 a total of 933 squadrons remained active, with 868 assigned to the various groups. 65 squadrons, mostly <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Night_fighter" title="Night fighter">night fighter</a>, were not assigned to groups but as separate units under higher command echelons.<sup id="cite_ref-maurer8_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maurer8-104">[77]</a></sup></p>

<td align="center">18</td>
<td align="center">16</td>
<td align="center">8</td>
<td align="center">474</td>
<td align="center">77</td>
<td align="center">397</td>
</tr>
</table>
<dl>
<dd><small><sup>1</sup>Night fighter squadrons were not organized into groups.</small></dd>

</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft">Aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army_Air_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_military_aircraft_of_the_United_States" title="List of military aircraft of the United States">List of military aircraft of the United States</a></div>
<p>The United States Army Air Forces used a large variety of aircraft in accomplishing its various missions, including many obsolete aircraft left over from its pre-June 1941 time as the Air Corps, with fifteen designations of types.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[n 56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following were the most numerous types in the USAAF inventory, or those that specifically saw combat. Variants, including all photo-reconnaissance ("F") variants, are listed and described under their separate articles. Many aircraft, particularly transports and trainers, had numerous designations resulting from differences in power plants.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bomber">Bomber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army_Air_Forces&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bomber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
